        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.7b15659"><meta property="og:title" content="Facile Synthesis of ZnS/N,S Co-doped Carbon Composite from Zinc Metal Complex for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/acsami.7b15659.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="ZnS coated on N,S co-doped carbon (ZnS/NSC) composite has been prepared utilizing zinc pyrithione (C10H8N2O2S2Zn) as raw material via calcination. Through activation using Na2CO3 salt, ZnS nanoparticles encapsulated in NSC (denoted as A-ZnS/NSC) with mixed-crystal structure has also been obtained, which reveals much larger specific surface area and more bridges between ZnS and NSC. Based on the existence of bridges (C–S–Zn and S–O–Zn bonds) and the modification of carbon from N,S co-doping, the A-ZnS/NSC composite as an anode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) displays significantly enhanced electrochemical performances with a high reversible specific capacity of 516.6 mA h g–1 (at 100 mA g–1), outstanding cycling stability (96.9% capacity retention after 100 cycles at 100 mA g–1), and high rate behavior (364.9 mA h g–1 even at 800 mA g–1)."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/acsami.7b15659.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="931887bb8dbf54b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="931887bb8dbf54b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '61245936-cc47-4328-bcc2-ad7c032f3318'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="931887bb8dbf54b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

        <main data-widget-id="28284a6d-730d-4a56-93ad-909787c5ac1b" id="" class="content article"><article data-getftr-enabled="data-getFTR-enabled" data-getftr-references-enabled="data-getFTR-references-enabled" data-getftr-reference-url="/action/CitationEntitlement" data-getftr-citedby-enabled="data-getFTR-citedBy-enabled" data-references="https://pubs.acs.org/action/ajaxShowEnhancedAbstract?doi=10.1021%2Facsami.7b15659&amp;ajax=true" data-enable-mathjax="true" vocab="http://schema.org/" typeof="ScholarlyArticle" class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a href="#" title="Close" aria-label="close" tabindex="0" class="figure-viewer_close icon-close"></a><h2 class="figure-viewer_label"><span class="figure-viewer_current"></span></h2><div class="figure-viewer_image"><a href="#" title="Next" class="figure-viewer_next icon-angle-right"></a><a href="#" title="Previous" class="figure-viewer_prev icon-angle-left"></a><div class="figure-viewer_image-content"><img src="" alt="Figure 1" class="fv-img"><img src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/images/loading/loading-018624cffd023ad5641b8e99931a80e6.gif" alt="Loading Img" class="loading-img"></div></div><div class="figure-viewer_links"><a href="#" title="Download Hi-Res Image" target="_blank" class="figure-viewer_hi-res-link"><i class="icon-file-image-o"></i><span>Download Hi-Res Image</span></a><span class="fv-separator"></span><a href="#" title="Download to MS-PowerPoint" class="figure-viewer_ms-p-link"><i class="icon-file-powerpoint-o"></i><span>Download to MS-PowerPoint</span></a><span class="cit-this-container"><a href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b15659&amp;href=/doi/10.1021/acsami.7b15659" title="Cite This" class="figure-viewer-cite-this-link"><i class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite This:</strong></span></a><span class="article_header-cite-this figure-viewer-cite-this"><span class="cit-fg-title"><i>ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces</i></span><span class="cit-fg-year"> 2018</span><span class="cit-fg-volume">, 10</span><span class="cit-fg-issue">, 1</span><span class="cit-fg-pageRange">, 704-712</span></span></span></div></div></div></div></div><input type="hidden" name="aamick" id="copyRights" value="/pb-assets/json/journal-info-1744387780883.json"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone5" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone5"><!-- Article Refresh Event Tracking -->
<script nonce="931887bb8dbf54b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Facile Synthesis of ZnS/N,S Co-doped Carbon Composite from Zinc Metal Complex for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b15659", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Facile Synthesis of ZnS/N,S Co-doped Carbon Composite from Zinc Metal Complex for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b15659", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Facile Synthesis of ZnS/N,S Co-doped Carbon Composite from Zinc Metal Complex for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b15659", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Facile Synthesis of ZnS/N,S Co-doped Carbon Composite from Zinc Metal Complex for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b15659", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Facile Synthesis of ZnS/N,S Co-doped Carbon Composite from Zinc Metal Complex for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b15659", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Facile Synthesis of ZnS/N,S Co-doped Carbon Composite from Zinc Metal Complex for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b15659", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Facile Synthesis of ZnS/N,S Co-doped Carbon Composite from Zinc Metal Complex for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b15659", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Facile Synthesis of ZnS/N,S Co-doped Carbon Composite from Zinc Metal Complex for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b15659", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Facile Synthesis of ZnS/N,S Co-doped Carbon Composite from Zinc Metal Complex for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b15659", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/medium/am-2017-156594_0009.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/large/am-2017-156594_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b15659&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">ZnS coated on N,S co-doped carbon (ZnS/NSC) composite has been prepared utilizing zinc pyrithione (C<sub>10</sub>H<sub>8</sub>N<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub>Zn) as raw material via calcination. Through activation using Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> salt, ZnS nanoparticles encapsulated in NSC (denoted as A-ZnS/NSC) with mixed-crystal structure has also been obtained, which reveals much larger specific surface area and more bridges between ZnS and NSC. Based on the existence of bridges (C–S–Zn and S–O–Zn bonds) and the modification of carbon from N,S co-doping, the A-ZnS/NSC composite as an anode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) displays significantly enhanced electrochemical performances with a high reversible specific capacity of 516.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>), outstanding cycling stability (96.9% capacity retention after 100 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>), and high rate behavior (364.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> even at 800 mA g<sup>–1</sup>).</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D704%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DMingjun%2BJing%252C%2BZhengu%2BChen%252C%2BZhi%2BLi%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D10%26issueNum%3D1%26contentID%3Dacsami.7b15659%26title%3DFacile%2BSynthesis%2Bof%2BZnS%252FN%252CS%2BCo-doped%2BCarbon%2BComposite%2Bfrom%2BZinc%2BMetal%2BComplex%2Bfor%2BHigh-Performance%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D712%26publicationDate%3DJanuary%2B2018" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2017 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291331&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Composites"> Composites </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292506&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Materials"> Materials </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290920&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanoparticles"> Nanoparticles </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291399&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Zinc"> Zinc </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Zns%2Fcarbon+Composite&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_ZnS%2Fcarbon%20composite">ZnS/carbon composite</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=N%2CS+Co-doped&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_N%2CS%20co-doped">N,S co-doped</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Zinc+Pyrithione&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_zinc%20pyrithione">zinc pyrithione</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Bridges&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_bridges">bridges</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">1 Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted considerable attention as one of the most promising candidates for electrical energy storage, owing to the high abundant sodium element from cheap sources and decent energy densities. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1, 2)</a> Recently, metal sulfides (including layered and nonlayered) with high theoretical specific capacity display greater performance over other anode materials for SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> Nonlayered metal sulfides (such as FeS<sub>2</sub>, CoS<sub>2</sub>, NiS<sub>2</sub>, CuS, ZnS, MnS, and so on) are very popular because of their low price, compared with layered metal sulfides (for example, MoS<sub>2</sub>, WS<sub>2</sub>, and SnS<sub>2</sub>, etc.). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 3 to 5">(3-5)</a> Particularly, nontoxic ZnS as anode exhibits a high initial Coulombic efficiency (above 70%, vs about 40% of carbon material), which is popularizing and drawing more attention for SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The proposed sodiation/desodiation mechanism of ZnS includes conversion reaction, ZnS + 2Na + 2e<sup>–</sup> ↔ Na<sub>2</sub>S + Zn, and alloying/dealloying reaction, 13Zn + Na<sup>+</sup> + e<sup>–</sup> ↔ NaZn<sub>13</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 7 and 8">(7, 8)</a> During the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction processes, there is a large volume expansion in ZnS electrode, which becomes one of the bottlenecks in the development of ZnS for SIB applications. Compared to Li-ion batteries (LIBs), the volume change in SIBs is more severe because of the large ionic radius, 1.02 Å, of Na<sup>+</sup> (about 55% larger than that of Li<sup>+</sup>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 9 and 10">(9, 10)</a> Another problem of the ZnS anode material is its poor electrical conductivity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> Due to these drawbacks, pure ZnS always suffers from poor rate and cycling stability. Based on studies about some similar materials, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2 ref6 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref2 ref6 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 2, 6 and 12 to 17">(2, 6, 12-17)</a> the following strategies are used to solve these problems: (i) utilizing other electrolytes (such as organic liquid, ether-based electrolytes, and so on) to improve the rate of ion diffusion and electrochemical reversibility, (ii) designing unique morphologic structures to remit volume expansion, and (iii) constructing composite with high electrical conductivity material, especially various carbon materials. Among these approaches, the third method is regarded as one of the most effective ways. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 18 and 19">(18, 19)</a> Carbon materials, as a conducting matrix and a mechanical cushion, can enhance the electron transport and buffer the volume change of ZnS particles. For example, ZnS–GO composite prepared via a microwave-assisted method displays a much better capacity retention (88%) than that of pure ZnS nanoparticles (15.6%). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Doping and co-doping with heteroatom (e.g., N, B, S, and P) have been applied to effectively improve the physicochemical properties of carbon. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10 ref20" aria-label="reference 9, 10 and 20">(9, 10, 20)</a> For example, N doping can improve the electronic conductivities and Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacities of various carbon materials due to the introduction of N-doped defects and functionalized groups. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> S-doped carbon can significantly expand the interlayer distance to promote the insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions on account of the larger covalent radius of S (102 pm) compared with that of C (77 pm). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> It is stressed that co-doping may provide more outstanding performances attributed to the synergetic effect. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 10, 23 and 24">(10, 23, 24)</a> Therefore, N,S co-doped carbonaceous materials have been applied to construct composites with ZnS, which might be an operative approach to solve the drawbacks of ZnS as an anode for SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">Currently, two main synthetic approaches have been utilized to prepare ZnS/carbon composites. In the first approach, ZnS materials are first obtained and then coated with different carbon materials using various carbon precursors such as glucose, polydopamine, and so on. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref25" aria-label="reference 7 and 25">(7, 25)</a> In the second approach, various kinds of carbon materials (GO, carbon nanotube, and so on) are added into the system which includes Zn and S sources to grow ZnS. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 26 and 27">(26, 27)</a> However, these methods always suffer from tricky multistep processes and high cost. In addition, preparation of N,S co-doped carbonaceous materials normally need to introduce extra N and S sources, such as thiourea, 1-allyl-2-thiourea, 2-aminothiophenol, polyrhodanine, and so on. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29 ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28 ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 28 to 30">(28-30)</a> If the precursors of carbon contained N and S, the preparation process would become simpler. Mullins et al. got Sn/NC via a simple clinkering process using SnCl<sub>4</sub> mixed with nitrilotriacetic acid. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> Based on the above investigations, we design a facial approach to obtain the ZnS/N,S co-doped carbon (ZnS/NSC) composite utilizing low-cost Zn metal complexes as raw material.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, the ZnS/NSC composite has been first prepared through direct calcination of zinc pyrithione (zinc pyridine-2-thiol-<i>N</i>-oxide) that is a common Zn metal complex used for over 50 years in personal hygiene. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> The zinc pyrithione served as the single source for both ZnS and NSC. Furthermore, activated ZnS/NSC (A-ZnS/NSC) has been also gotten via the same process with the addition of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> salt. More significantly, the effective chemical bonding between ZnS and NSC can help to enhance cycling stability and rate behavior of as-prepared composite for SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">2 Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i3" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i3">2.1 Synthesis of Active Materials</h3><div class="NLM_p last">ZnS/NSC composite was prepared using thermally treated zinc pyrithione (zinc pyridine-2-thiol-<i>N</i>-oxide, C<sub>10</sub>H<sub>8</sub>N<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub>Zn, Sigma-Aldrich, ≥95%). Zinc pyrithione (2.50 g), a white powder, was calcined under Ar flow at 600 °C for 2 h with the heating rate of 5 °C/min. Then, the freshly as-prepared powder was washed by utilizing distilled water (350 mL) and ethanol (50 mL) and dried at 80 °C for 12 h, to get black ZnS/NSC composite. The heat-treatment temperature (600 °C) has been determined based on the analysis of various products prepared at various temperatures (450, 600, and 750 °C). <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/suppl_file/am7b15659_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a> Figure S1 illustrates the size of ZnS becomes bigger with rising temperatures. The cycling performances of these samples were also measured at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, which is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/suppl_file/am7b15659_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>. It displays that the sample obtained at 600 °C shows the best electrochemical performance. With increasing temperature of calcination, the carbon content in composite decreases. So, the capacity of sample (at 450 °C) is lower due to the higher carbon content. The electrochemical performances of ZnS/NSC are related to the size of ZnS and carbon contents. In addition, A-ZnS/NSC was synthesized similarly with zinc pyrithione first mixed with Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (<i>m</i>(zinc pyrithione):<i>m</i>(Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>) = 1:1.5) at 600 °C for 2 h.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.2 Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The crystal structure patterns were carried out by X-ray diffraction (XRD) with Rigaku D/max 2550 VB<sup>+</sup> at a scanning rate of 0.1° 2θ s<sup>–1</sup>. The morphologies and structural characteristics were investigated via utilizing scanning electron microscopy (SEM; JSM-6510LV), transmission electron microscopy (TEM; JEM-2100F), and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM; JEM-2100F). Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) was conducted within the temperature range from 25 to 900 °C on a thermal analysis instrument (NETZSCH STA449F3) at a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup> in air. Specific surface areas were gotten via N<sub>2</sub> sorption/desorption analysis on a Micromeritics ASAP 2020 HD analyzer at 77 K. The surface composition analysis was further researched by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS; ESCALab250) with C 1s at 284.6 eV as a reference. A Fourier transform infrared spectrophotometer (FT-IR; AVTATAR, 370) was utilized to collect FT-IR spectra of samples with KBr as a reference.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.3 Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The working electrodes were obtained through a typical synthesis process. Active materials, carboxymethylcellulose (CMC) binder, and super P (at a 75:15:10 weight ratio) were mixed in deionized water. The formed slurries were then homogeneously coated on copper foils and dried at 100 °C for 12 h in vacuum. The electrodes were further pressed under a pressure of about 15 MPa. The mass loading is 1.0–1.2 mg/cm<sup>2</sup>. CR2025 coin cells were fabricated in an Ar-filled glovebox utilizing a metallic sodium foil as counter and reference electrodes, a solution of NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate and propylene carbonate (1:1 in volume) as electrolyte, and a porous polypropylene film as the separator. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests at a scanning rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> were conducted on MULTI AUTOLAB M204 (MAC90086). Electrochemical impedance measurements (EIS) with the frequency range of 100 kHz to 0.01 Hz at their open-circuit voltages were conducted on a CHI 660B electrochemical working station. Galvanostatic charge/discharge files between 0.01 and 3.0 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) were investigated using a Land CT2001A battery cycler.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">3 Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a shows the XRD patterns of as-prepared ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC samples. All diffraction peaks of ZnS/NSC can be assigned to the hexagonal ZnS containing the 6H phase (JCPDS No. 89-2191), indicating that the Zn(II) in the molecular structure of zinc pyrithione is successfully turned into ZnS substance without impurity through a calcination process. The diffraction peaks at A-ZnS/NSC material mainly correspond to the hexagonal structure (2H phase, JCPDS No. 75-1547), while the existence of weak peaks at 36.1° and 69.4° can be indexed to the 6H phase (JCPDS No. 89-2191), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> meaningfully noting that the crystalline structure of A-ZnS/NSC material is a hexagonal structure that contains 2H and 6H polytypes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> The corresponding ball-and-stick models are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b. Like other materials, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 33 and 34">(33, 34)</a> this mixed-phase could promote the electrical transmission rate. So, this structure could contribute to improvement of the electrical conductivity of ZnS itself. In addition, the related diffraction peaks of NSC have not been observed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a. This illustrates the amorphous state NSC was obtained from zinc pyrithione via calcination.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/medium/am-2017-156594_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/large/am-2017-156594_0001.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) XRD patterns of ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC samples. (b) Ball-and-stick models of 2H and 6H of ZnS polytypes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/large/am-2017-156594_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b15659&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a> displays the SEM images of ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC composites. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a,c, irregular ZnS particles in ZnS/NSC sample are mainly distributed on the surface of NSC material. The size distribution of ZnS particles in ZnS/NSC (the inset of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a) illustrates the size of ZnS particles obtained in ZnS/NSC is inhomogeneous and the average size is about 150 nm, while the A-ZnS/NSC exhibits carbon-encapsulated ZnS nanoparticles as seen from the SEM in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b,d. The main size of ZnS nanoparticles in A-ZnS/NSC is about 60 nm based on the analysis of the inset of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b, illustrating the size of ZnS becomes small with the introduction of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>. The smaller size can provide more active sites for electrochemical reaction and the carbon-encapsulated structure might be more effective to relieve volume expansion during the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion process. The compositions of ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC were further analyzed by energy dispersive X-ray (EDX) in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/suppl_file/am7b15659_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S3b and S4b</a>. It reveals that the two composites are both comprised of Zn, S, C, and N elements. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/suppl_file/am7b15659_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S3 and S4</a> display the energy dispersive X-ray spectrometry mapping analysis of ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC samples to estimate the distribution of elements. Panels c–f of <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/suppl_file/am7b15659_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a> show that Zn, S, and C elements are uniformly and continuously distributed with a small amount of N element, confirming again the components of ZnS/NSC sample. Similar results in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/suppl_file/am7b15659_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4c–f</a> suggest that the A-ZnS/NSC composite contains ZnS and NSC. All of the above results illustrate ZnS decorated with N,S co-doped carbon is obtained by calcinating zinc pyrithione without other C, N, or S sources.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/medium/am-2017-156594_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/large/am-2017-156594_0002.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. SEM images of (a and c) ZnS/NSC and (b and d) A-ZnS/NSC. The insert pictures correspond to the size distributions of ZnS particles in ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC composites, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/large/am-2017-156594_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b15659&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">TEM and HRTEM images of ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC have been further conducted to investigate the morphology and microstructure of samples, which were displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>. Compared with <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a,b, the morphology structure of A-ZnS/NSC is obviously different from that of ZnS/NSC. Based on the chemical activation of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, carbon-encapsulated ZnS nanoparticles were formed. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c shows the HRTEM image of ZnS/NSC. The lattice fringes of 0.310 nm are from the (006) plane of 6H crystalline ZnS, and the as-formed NSC is amorphous. In the HRTEM image of A-ZnS/NSC (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d), the lattice fringes at 0.332 and 0.137 nm correspond to the (100) phase of the 2H crystal and the (208) phase of the 6H crystal, respectively, which is in perfect accordance with XRD results. Also, the NSC structure in A-ZnS/NSC composite is an amorphous state.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/medium/am-2017-156594_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/large/am-2017-156594_0003.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. TEM and HRTEM images of (a and c, respectively) ZnS/NSC and (b and d, respectively) A-ZnS/NSC.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/large/am-2017-156594_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b15659&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Moreover, the contents of NSC in ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC composites were investigated via TGA operated from 25 to 900 °C, which is shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a. The first step of weight less samples from 25 to 150 °C is mainly resulted from adsorbed water. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref11" aria-label="reference 7 and 11">(7, 11)</a> The following step is due to less weight from coordinated water and oxygen-containing groups in the samples. The third less weight step from 400 to 750 °C is attributed to both oxidation process of ZnS (ZnS + (3/2)O<sub>2</sub> → ZnO + SO<sub>2</sub>) and combustion of NSC. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> Based on the final product of ZnO, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> the contents of ZnS component in ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC can be calculated to be approximately 86.9 and 78.7 wt %, respectively, according to the following equation (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a>):<span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq1"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/am-2017-156594_m001.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></span>Therefore, more carbon could remain with the addition of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> during the calcination of zinc pyrithione. From the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b, the specific surface area (SSA) of ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC materials are determined to be 13.5 and 59.2 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The larger SSA of A-ZnS/NSC composite is likely attributed to the smaller size of ZnS nanoparticles and higher content of NSC.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/medium/am-2017-156594_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/large/am-2017-156594_0004.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) TGA curves of ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC samples. (b) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms of ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC samples.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/large/am-2017-156594_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b15659&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further confirm the chemical states of as-gotten materials, the surface chemical status of elements in ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC were determined by XPS. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a, the XPS survey spectra of ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC samples indicate the presence of Zn, S, C, and N, which is well in accordance with the EDX mapping analysis. The Zn 2p spectra are presented in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b. The two peaks at 1021.8 and 1044.8 eV correspond to Zn 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Zn 2p<sub>3/2</sub> peaks of Zn<sup>2+</sup>, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> Panels c and d of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a> display the S 2p spectra of ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC. Each spectrum contains eight fitted peaks. The two major peaks at 161.5 and 162.5 eV are indexed to S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> in the ZnS phase, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> Another two peaks at 163.5 eV (S 2p<sub>3/2</sub>) and 164.5 eV (S 2p<sub>1/2</sub>) are assigned to the C–S–C covalent bond. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref37 ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 23, 37 and 38">(23, 37, 38)</a> The weak peaks from 167 to 170.5 eV are from the C–SO<sub><i>x</i></sub>–C sulfone bridges. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> The N 1s spectra of ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/suppl_file/am7b15659_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>. Two fitted peaks at 398.5 and 400.3 eV can be assigned to pyridinic-N and pyrrolic-N, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref21" aria-label="reference 9 and 21">(9, 21)</a> Therefore, as-formed carbon is successfully doped by N and S in both ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC samples. It is expected that the electronic conductivity and total electrochemical capacity storage of samples can be effectively improved because much more defects and active sites could be introduced to the carbon framework through N and S co-doping. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref30 ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref30 ref39" aria-label="reference 10, 30 and 39">(10, 30, 39)</a></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/medium/am-2017-156594_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/large/am-2017-156594_0005.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) XPS survey spectra of ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC samples. (b) Zn 2p XPS spectra of ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC. (c) S 2p XPS spectrum of ZnS/NSC. The inset is magnification from 166.5 to 170.5 eV. (d) S 2p XPS spectrum of A-ZnS/NSC. The inset is magnification from 166.5 to 170.5 eV.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/large/am-2017-156594_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b15659&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">From the two S 2p spectra, the A-ZnS/NSC composite exhibits much more C–SO<sub><i>x</i></sub>–C bands, which might be due to the existence of the extra O source from Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>. Interestingly, the area ratio of S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> (161.5 eV) and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> (162.5 eV) in A-ZnS/NSC is lower than that in ZnS/NSC. Based on the normal ratio (2:1, in pure ZnS phase) and previous preliminary explorations, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> it is believed that the smaller ratio might be mainly attributed to the mixed-crystal structure and the stronger interaction between C–S (meaning the possible formation of C–S–Zn bond). Compared to that of the ZnS/NSC composite, the binding energy values of C–SO<sub><i>x</i></sub>–C band significantly increased in A-ZnS/NSC composite, from the insets in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>. This can be contributed to the possible formation of S–O–Zn bonds. Therefore, there is a stronger interaction between ZnS and NSC in the A-ZnS/NSC hybrid via the bridges of C–S–Zn and S–O–Zn, which might be probably indispensable for the outstanding electrochemical properties for SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">FT-IR spectra of zinc pyrithione, ZnS/NSC, and A-ZnS/NSC are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/suppl_file/am7b15659_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>. After calcination, the characteristic peaks of zinc pyrithione vanished. The characteristic vibrations at about 636 cm<sup>–1</sup> were still maintained, indicating the formation of ZnS. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> The weak bands at 1200–1600 cm<sup>–1</sup> are indexed to stretching modes of aromatic C–N and C–S. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 41 and 42">(41, 42)</a> Compared with that of ZnS/NSC, the stronger peak at 636 cm<sup>–1</sup> of A-ZnS/NSC could be due to the effect of more C–S–Zn bonds. All these results are well in accordance with the XRD and XPS analysis.</div><div class="NLM_p">In order to better realize these structural changes, the possible fabricating process of samples is shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>. Pyrolysis of zinc pyrithione could be the first process during the heat-treatment under Ar. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> Then, the Zn-based products were transformed gradually into ZnS nanoparticles and the products including pyrithione were turned into NSC. Finally, the ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC composites were obtained, which have been confirmed by XRD and HRTEM results. The main functions of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> in preparation are embodied from two aspects. On the one hand, the weakly alkaline Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> may accelerate decomposition to form small ZnS nanoparticles. On the other hand, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> can be used as a template for carbon framework and ZnS, like other salts previous reported. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44 ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 44 and 45">(44, 45)</a> Hence, the carbon-encapsulated structure of A-ZnS/NSC with the smaller size of ZnS was formed. Above all, A-ZnS/NSC with larger SSA and stronger interaction could expose better electrochemical behaviors than those of ZnS/NSC.</div><figure id="sch1" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/medium/am-2017-156594_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/large/am-2017-156594_0008.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. Schematic Representation of ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC Materials</div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/large/am-2017-156594_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b15659&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The sodium-ion storage actions of the as-obtained ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC composites were investigated in sodium half-cells. CV profiles were first measured with the potential range from 0.01 to 3 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) at a scanning rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, which is shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a,b. Similar curves of the two composites illustrate similar electrochemical reactions. The broad peak between 0.2 and 1.0 V during the first cathodic scan is attributed to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into NSC and ZnS, the electrochemical reaction of ZnS, and the formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> During the following anodic scanning, a wide anodic peak at about 0.8 V is observed, suggesting that Na<sup>+</sup> is extracted by several steps (mainly include dealloying reactions). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> And a strong oxidation peak at about 1.0 V can be ascribed to the conversion reaction. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> In the second scanning cycle, the cathodic peaks below 0.6 V primarily correspond to the reduction of ZnS to metallic Zn and the further alloying reaction with multiple steps. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> Meanwhile, the subsequent CV profiles are overlapped, declaring the good electrochemical reversibility of the working electrodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a></div><figure id="fig6" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/medium/am-2017-156594_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/large/am-2017-156594_0006.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. CV curves of (a) ZnS/NSC electrode at 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> and (b) A-ZnS/NSC electrode at 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. First and second galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles of (c) ZnS/NSC at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and (d) A-ZnS/NSC at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/large/am-2017-156594_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b15659&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The first and second charge/discharge profiles of the as-prepared samples are presented in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c,d, at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The first discharge capacity and Coulombic efficiency of ZnS/NSC composite are 896.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and 65.9%, respectively. And the first discharge capacity and Coulombic efficiency are 839.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and 60.9% for A-ZnS/NSC composite, respectively. The second discharge capacities of ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC electrodes are 561.2 and 516.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The irreversible capacity loss is mainly due to the irreversible formation of the SEI layer. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> While A-ZnS/NSC shows a lower discharge specific capacity in the initial cycle than that of ZnS/NSC electrode, which could be due to the higher content of carbon. Compared with some previous reported ZnS-based materials (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#tbl1" aria-label="Table 1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>), both ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC display high specific capacity for SIBs. The outstanding storage capacity might mainly benefit from N and S co-doping synergistic effect.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1.
