align="left">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">&nbsp;</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">The cyclic and rate performances of as-gained ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC materials are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>. As seen from <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a, the reversible specific capacities of ZnS/NSC electrode are higher than those of A-ZnS/NSC electrode during the initial six cycles. Generally, the capacity of carbon is lower than that of ZnS (theoretical specific capacity, 574 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref47" aria-label="reference 7 and 47">(7, 47)</a> The initial capacity of ZnS/NSC is higher than that of A-ZnS/NSC electrode, which is mainly due to the lower ZnS content in the A-ZnS/NSC composite, while the following reversible specific capacities of A-ZnS/NSC are higher than ZnS/NSC, illustrating the A-ZnS/NSC with larger specific surface area and more bridges exhibits much better electrochemical performances. After 100 cycles at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the discharge capacities of ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC electrodes are 379.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (about 67.7% capacity retention) and 500.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (about 96.9% capacity retention), respectively. Hence, A-ZnS/NSC exhibits better cycle stability than ZnS/NSC, indicating that the carbon-encapsulated structure can preferably remit the volume expansion of ZnS component during the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> Moreover, the cycling stability of A-ZnS/NSC composite at a high current density of 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> is shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b. The reversible specific capacity maintains 334.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (about 81.5% retention rate) after 200 galvanostatic charge–discharge cycles. The Coulombic efficiency quickly increased to 98.2% after eight cycles, suggesting splendid reversibility of electrochemical reaction from A-ZnS/NSC electrode.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/medium/am-2017-156594_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/large/am-2017-156594_0007.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a) Cycling performances of ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC samples at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Cycling performance and Coulombic efficiency of A-ZnS/NSC at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Rate performances of ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC samples at different current densities of 100, 200, 400, 800, and 1600 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. (d) Nyquist plots of electrodes after 100 cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/asset/images/large/am-2017-156594_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b15659&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>c displays the rate capabilities of as-prepared samples. A-ZnS/NSC composite shows much better rate behavior than that of ZnS/NSC. The discharge specific capacities of A-ZnS/NSC composite are 514.8, 465.9, 421.7, 364.9, and 316.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the current densities of 100, 200, 400, 800, and 1600 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. When the current density got back to 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the discharge specific capacity returned to 507.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. The better performances may be mainly attributed to the mixed-crystal structure of A-ZnS/NSC, the larger SSA, and the stronger interaction through bridges between ZnS and NSC. The EIS measurements of electrodes were further conducted at the open-circuit voltages after 100 cycles, which is presented in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>d. Compared with semicircle shapes, the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) of A-ZnS/NSC composite is smaller than that of ZnS/NSC composite, confirming the A-ZnS/NSC exhibits a much higher charge transfer rate. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">Taking ZnS-based materials as anodes for SIBs reported so far for comparison (in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#tbl1" aria-label="Table 1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>), the as-prepared A-ZnS/NSC sample shows outstanding cycling stability and rate behavior, which is mainly contributed to the following two factors: (i) N and S co-doping and (ii) bridges between ZnS and NSC. According to the XPS results, the amounts of N and S dopants in the surface A-ZnS/NSC composite are about 2.8 and 2.1 wt %, respectively. For the carbon framework, the N and S co-doping can enhance the electrical conductivity, beneficial for promoting the electron transfer rate of the electrode. Also, more defects and active sites could be introduced to improve the total capacity of the composite because of N and S co-doping. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> Moreover, the existence of C–S–Zn and S–O–Zn bonds in the composite confirmed by XPS analysis is probably helpful for the excellent electrochemical performances. The strong interaction between ZnS and NSC can effectively decrease the aggregation of ZnS nanoparticles during the preparation and cyclical processes. The carbon-encapsulated structure of the hybrid may better tolerate the large volume expansion of the ZnS nanoparticles during the intercalation/deintercalation.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">4 Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, ZnS/NSC composite is prepared by direct calcination of zinc pyrithione. With the introduction of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, a larger specific surface area, a higher carbon content, and more bridges (C–S–Zn and S–O–Zn bonds) were obtained in A-ZnS/NSC composite. N,S co-doped carbon is beneficial for enhancing the electron transfer rate and improving the specific capacity of composites. The A-ZnS/NSC composite as an anode for SIBs displays much better cycling stability (500.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> of discharge capacity and about 96.9% capacity retention after 100 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) and rate behavior (364.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> even at a high current density of 800 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) than those of ZnS/NSC composite, which is mainly due to its mixed-crystal and carbon-encapsulated structure, and the strong interaction between ZnS and NSC. Meaningfully, metal sulfide/carbon composites prepared utilizing sulfur-containing metal complexes as raw material could exhibit strong interaction between metal sulfide and carbon, which might become one effective approach to improve the electrochemical performances for SIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.7b15659" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.7b15659</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM images of samples obtained at different heat-treatment temperatures and after cycles; cycling performances of samples under various heat-treatment temperatures; EDX spectra, element mapping, and N 1s XPS of ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC; and FT-IR spectra of zinc pyrithione, ZnS/NSC, and A-ZnS/NSC (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/suppl_file/am7b15659_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/suppl_file/am7b15659_si_001.pdf">am7b15659_si_001.pdf (1.45 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.7b15659" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Pyrite (FeS2) nanocrystals as inexpensive high-performance lithium-ion cathode and sodium-ion anode materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Walter, Marc; Zund, Tanja; Kovalenko, Maksym V.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nanoscale</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">20</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">9158-9163</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NANOHL</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2040-3372</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In light of the impeding depletion of fossil fuels and necessity to lower carbon dioxide emissions, economically viable high-performance batteries are urgently needed for numerous applications ranging from elec. cars to stationary large-scale electricity storage.  Due to its low raw material cost, non-toxicity and potentially high charge-storage capacity pyrite (FeS2) is a highly promising material for such next-generation batteries.  In this work we present the electrochem. performance of FeS2 nanocrystals (NCs) as lithium-ion and sodium-ion storage materials.  First, we show that nanoscopic FeS2 is a promising lithium-ion cathode material, delivering a capacity of 715 mA h g-1 and av. energy d. of 1237 Wh kg-1 for 100 cycles, twice higher than for commonly used LiCoO2 cathodes.  Then we demonstrate, for the first time, that FeS2 NCs can serve as highly reversible sodium-ion anode material with long cycling life.  As sodium-ion anode material, FeS2 NCs provide capacities above 500 mA h g-1 for 400 cycles at a current rate of 1000 mA g-1.  In all our tests and control expts., the performance of chem. synthesized nanoscale FeS2 clearly surpasses bulk FeS2 as well as large no. of other nanostructured metal sulfides.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2016.06.019"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Song, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bai, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yan, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sun, X.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Morphology-Dependent Performance of Nanostructured Ni<sub>3</sub>S<sub>2</sub>/Ni Anode Electrodes for High Performance Sodium Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nano Energy</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">26</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">533</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">540</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.nanoen.2016.06.019</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D26%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D533-540%26journal%3DNano%2BEnergy%26author%3DX.%2BSong%26author%3DX.%2BLi%26author%3DZ.%2BBai%26author%3DB.%2BYan%26author%3DD.%2BLi%26author%3DX.%2BSun%26title%3DMorphology-Dependent%2BPerformance%2Bof%2BNanostructured%2BNi3S2%252FNi%2BAnode%2BElectrodes%2Bfor%2BHigh%2BPerformance%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.nanoen.2016.06.019&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.7b15659&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.nanoen.2016.06.019&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Morphology-dependent performance of nanostructured Ni3S2/Ni anode electrodes for high performance sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Song, Xiaosheng; Li, Xifei; Bai, Zhimin; Yan, Bo; Li, Dejun; Sun, Xueliang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">26</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">533-540</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NEANCA</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Transition metal sulfides have been treated as promising materials for lithium-ion battery, and recently more and more attention has been paid to its applications in sodium-ion batteries.  In our context, three Ni3S2 nanostructures directly grown on Ni foam were successfully designed using a facile hydrothermal method.  The influences of the morphol. on the performance evolution for sodium-ion batteries were studied in detail.  As a result, it was found that the initial drop, gradual increase, convex type decline and concave type decline in the performance evolutions were mainly controlled by Rct, Rsf, pulverization, and internal stress, resp.  More importantly, our results indicated that the clustered network-like structure was beneficial for the cycling and rate performance, while the rod-like structure was suitable for the cyclic stability of these electrodes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201501785"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Su, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kretschmer, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, G.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Improved Electrochemical Performance of Na-Ion Batteries in Ether-Based Electrolytes: A Case Study of ZnS Nanospheres</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Urchinlike ZnS Microspheres Decorated with Nitrogen-Doped Carbon: A Superior Anode Material for Lithium and Sodium Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Junming; Fu, Yun; Shi, Xiaodong; Xu, Zhenming; Zhang, Zhian</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry - A European Journal</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Urchinlike ZnS microspheres decorated with nitrogen-doped carbon (ZnS@NC) were fabricated by a facile two-step method in which urchinlike ZnS microspheres were coated with polydopamine and then calcined in an inert gas atm.  When employed as the anode material for lithium-ion batteries and sodium ion batteries, ZnS@NC exhibits a reversible lithium-storage capacity of 690 mAh g-1 after 100 cycles at 100 mA g-1 and a reversible sodium-storage capacity of 460 mAh g-1 after 80 cycles at 200 mA g-1.  The ZnS@NC electrode shows a high reversible lithium-storage capacity of 520 mAh g-1 after 200 cycles and a high reversible sodium-storage capacity of 380 mAh g-1 after 100 cycles even at a c.d. of 1 A g-1.  The superior electrochem. performance could be ascribed to structural merits of the urchinlike ZnS microspheres and synergistic effects between ZnS and polydopamine-derived nitrogen-doped carbon.  The lithium- and sodium-storage capacities of urchinlike ZnS@NC microspheres are in the top rank in comparison with those reported in other studies.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2016.01.116"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qin, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yan, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Pan, L.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">ZnS Nanoparticles Embedded in Reduced Graphene Oxide as High Performance Anode Material of Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Electrochim.         </div><div class="casAbstract">ZnS nanoparticles embedded in reduced graphene oxide (RGO) were prepd. via a facile microwave-assisted method and applied as anode materials of Na-ion batteries (SIBs).  The as-prepd. composites exhibit excellent Na storage properties.  A max. specific capacity of 481 mAh g-1 at the specific current of 100 mA g-1 can be achieved after 50 cycles by optimizing the RGO content in the composites.  The improved Na-ion storage ability can be ascribed to the enhanced sp. surface area and increased elec. cond. due to the introduction of RGO and the decreased size of ZnS particles which can shorten the pathway of Na-ion diffusion and facilitate the reversible Na+ diffusion kinetics.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201300071"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhou, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wan, L.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nanoconfined antimony in sulfur and nitrogen co-doped three-dimensionally (3D) interconnected macroporous carbon for high-performance sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Chenglong; Li, Weihan; Yang, Zhenzhong; Gu, Lin; Yu, Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The Sb encapsulated in S and N co-doped three-dimensionally (3D) interconnected macroporous carbon composite (denoted as Sb@S,N-3DPC) has been fabricated by using silica opals as hard template following a carbonization process.  The structural and morphol. characterizations reveal the uniform 3D-network structure with the Sb nanoparticles embedded homogeneously in the carbon spherical shell.  The Sb@S,N-3DPC electrode shows a high reversible capacity of 482 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1, displaying good capacity stability (∼93% of the initial discharge capacity after 500 cycles at 100 mA g-1).  The electrode displays superior rate capability and ultralong cyclability (255 mAh g-1 at 2 Ag-1after 2000 cycles) and also reveals a good rate capability that maintains 160 mAh g-1 at 5 Ag-1.  The excellent electrochem. performance of the Sb@S,N-3DPC is attributed to the 3D-porous carbon network structure and uniform distribution of Sb nanoparticles in carbon matrix, enhancing the cond., holding the structure integrity, and thus improving the cycling performance of the anode material.  In addn., sulfur and nitrogen co-doping introduced more defects and active sites to the carbon framework, thereby improving the interfacial adsorption and electrochem. behaviors.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref11" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1039/C5TA01501D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mao, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jiang, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, T.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The Structure Control of ZnS/Graphene Composites and Their Excellent Properties for Lithium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">ZnS/graphene composites were prepd. using a facile solvothermal process by combining the redn. of graphene oxide with the growth of ZnS aggregates in one step.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref13" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1021/am504310k"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hou, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jing, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fang, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Song, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Pan, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ji, X.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium/Lithium Storage Behavior of Antimony Hollow Nanospheres for Rechargeable Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Appl.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium/Lithium Storage Behavior of Antimony Hollow Nanospheres for Rechargeable Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hou, Hongshuai; Jing, Mingjun; Yang, Yingchang; Zhu, Yirong; Fang, Laibing; Song, Weixin; Pan, Chengchi; Yang, Xuming; Ji, Xiaobo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries have come up as an alternative to lithium-ion batteries for large-scale applications because of abundant Na storage in the earth crust.  Antimony hollow nanospheres obtained by galvanic replacement were first applied as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries and exhibited superior electrochem. performances with high reversible capacity of 622.2 mA-h/g at a c.d. of 50 mA/g after 50 cycles, close to the theor. capacity (660 mA-h/g); even at high c.d. of 1600 mA/g, the reversible capacities can also reach 315 mA-h/g.  The benefits of this unique structure can also be extended to lithium-ion batteries, resulting in reversible capacity of 627.3 mA-h/g at a c.d. of 100 mA/g after 50 cycles, and at high c.d. of 1600 mA/g, the reversible capacity is 435.6 mA-h/g.  Thus, these benefits from the antimony hollow nanospheres are able to provide a robust architecture for anodes of sodium-ion batteries and lithium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref14" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.5b04474"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Song, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yu, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gordin, M.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Advanced Sodium Ion Battery Anode Constructed via Chemical Bonding between Phosphorus, Carbon Nanotube, and Cross-Linked Polymer Binder</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Song, Jiangxuan; Yu, Zhaoxin; Gordin, Mikhail L.; Li, Xiaolin; Peng, Huisheng; Wang, Donghai</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Maintaining structural stability is a great challenge for high-capacity conversion electrodes with large vol. change but is necessary for the development of high-energy-d., long-cycling batteries.  Here, a stable phosphorus anode is reported for sodium ion batteries by the synergistic use of chem. bonded phosphorus-carbon nanotube (P-CNT) hybrid and cross-linked polymer binder.  The P-CNT hybrid was synthesized through ball-milling of red phosphorus and carboxylic group functionalized carbon nanotubes.  The P-O-C bonds formed in this process help maintain contact between phosphorus and CNTs, leading to a durable hybrid.  In addn., cross-linked CM-cellulose-citric acid binder was used to form a robust electrode.  As a result, this anode delivers a stable cycling capacity of 1586.2 mAh/g after 100 cycles, along with high initial Coulombic efficiency of 84.7% and subsequent cycling efficiency of ∼99%.  The unique electrode framework through chem. bonding strategy reported here is potentially inspirable for other electrode materials with large vol. change in use.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref15" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1039/C5CC02564H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shadike, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ding, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sang, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fu, Z.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Improved Electrochemical Performance of CoS<sub>2</sub>–MWCNT Nanocomposites for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Well-controlled nanostructures and a high fraction of Sn/Li2O interface are crit. to enhance the coulombic efficiency and cyclic performance of SnO2-based electrodes for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).  Polydopamine (PDA)-coated SnO2 nanocrystals, composed of hundreds of PDA-coated corn-like SnO2 nanoparticles (diam. ca. 5 nm) decorated along a cob, addressed the irreversibility issue of SnO2-based electrodes.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The ability to create a synergistic effect of nanostructure engineering and its hybridization with conductive carbonaceous material is highly desirable for attaining high-performance lithium ion batteries (LIBs).  Herein, the ZnFe2O4/carbon nanocomposites composed of ZnFe2O4 nanoparticles with an av. size of 16 ± 5 nm encapsulated within the continuous carbon network as anode materials for LIBs are judiciously crafted.  Such intriguing nanocomposites were yielded in situ via the pyrolysis-induced carbonization of polystyrene@poly(acrylic acid) (PS@PAA) core@shell nanospheres in conjunction with the formation of ZnFe2O4 nanoparticles through the thermal decompn. of ZnFe2O4 precursors incorporated within the PS@PAA nanospheres.                         cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Large-Area Carbon Nanosheets Doped with Phosphorus: A High-Performance Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hou Hongshuai; Ji Xiaobo; Shao Lidong; Zhang Yan; Zou Guoqiang; Chen Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced science (Weinheim, Baden-Wurttemberg, Germany)</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Large-area phosphorus-doped carbon nanosheets (P-CNSs) are first obtained from carbon dots (CDs) through self-assembly driving from thermal treatment with Na catalysis.  This is the first time to realize the conversion from 0D CDs to 2D nanosheets doped with phosphorus.  The sodium storage behavior of phosphorus-doped carbon material is also investigated for the first time.  As anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), P-CNSs exhibit superb performances for electrochemical storage of sodium.  When cycled at 0.1 A g(-1), the P-CNSs electrode delivers a high reversible capacity of 328 mAh g(-1), even at a high current density of 20 A g(-1), a considerable capacity of 108 mAh g(-1) can still be maintained.  Besides, this material also shows excellent cycling stability, at a current density of 5 A g(-1), the reversible capacity can still reach 149 mAh g(-1) after 5000 cycles.  This work will provide significant value for the development of both carbon materials and SIBs anode materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref21" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2012.12.072"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qie, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yuan, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Huang, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Functionalized N-Doped Interconnected Carbon Nanofibers as an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Storage with Excellent Performance</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Carbon</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">55</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">328</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">334</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. performance of sodium-ion battery was improved by using functionalized interconnected N-doped carbon nanofibers (FN-CNFs) as the anode.  The material was synthesized with polypyrrole as precursor by a simple method.  The FN-CNF electrode exhibits excellent rate capability and cycling stability, delivering a capacity of 134.2 mAh/g at a high c.d. of 200 mA/g after 200 cycles and retains a capacity of 73 mAh/g even at an extremely high c.d. of 20 A/g. We report, for the first time, the graphene sheets covalently bonded with sulfur atoms as Na-ion battery anode material with a large reversible capacity of 291 mA h g-1 and an outstanding rate capability with 127 and 83 mA h g-1 at 2.0 and 5.0 A g-1 (charging/discharging in 3.8 and 1 min, resp.), and a long cycling stability as well.                         cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synergistically Enhanced Polysulfide Chemisorption Using a Flexible Hybrid Separator with N and S Dual-Doped Mesoporous Carbon Coating for Advanced Lithium-Sulfur Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Balach, Juan; Singh, Harish K.; Gomoll, Selina; Jaumann, Tony; Klose, Markus; Oswald, Steffen; Richter, Manuel; Eckert, Juergen; Giebeler, Lars</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Because of the outstanding high theor. specific energy d. of 2600 Wh kg-1, the lithium-sulfur (Li-S) battery is regarded as a promising candidate for post lithium-ion battery systems eligible to meet the forthcoming market requirements.  However, its commercialization on large scale is thwarted by fast capacity fading caused by the Achilles' heel of Li-S systems: the polysulfide shuttle.  Here, the phys. features are merged of carbon-coated separators and the unique chem. properties of N and S co-doped mesoporous carbon to create a functional hybrid separator with superior polysulfide affinity and electrochem. benefits.  DFT calcns. revealed that carbon materials with N and S co-doping possess a strong binding energy to high-order polysulfide species, which is essential to keep the active material in the cathode side.  As a result of the synergistic effect of N, S dual-doping, an advanced Li-S cell with high specific capacity and ultralow capacity degrdn. of 0.041% per cycle is achieved.  Pushing the simple-designed and scalable cathode to a highly increased sulfur loading of 5.4 mg cm-2, the Li-S cell with the functional hybrid separator can deliver a remarkable areal capacity of 5.9 mAh cm-2, which is highly favorable for practical applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref25" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1039/C4NR02368D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lim, Y.-F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ho, G.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The effect of graphene concn. on the photovoltaic and UV detector applications of ZnS/graphene nanocomposites was investigated. (5, 10, and 15 wt%) using L-cysteine amino acid as a surfactant and graphene oxide (GO) powder as a graphene source.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A facile self-sponsored doping approach is developed to synthesize S and N co-doped trimodal-porous structured graphitic carbon network electrocatalysts.  It utilizes a sole precursor (1-allyl-2-thiourea) to realize a precisely controlled co-doping of S and N during a concurrent graphitic carbon growth process by simple control of the pyrolysis temp.  The results reveal that the doping effect is heavily dependent on the doping d. and a maximal catalytic activity could only be achieved with an optimal doping level.  The presence of a macro-pore structure in the trimodal-porous network enhances the mass transport, enabling the full utilization of large surface areas created by micro- and meso-pores.  The resultant electrocatalyst possesses high ORR catalytic activity with excellent durability and high resistance to the inhibition effect of fuel mols.  The findings of this work would be valuable for design and fabrication of high performance carbon-based electrocatalysts.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref30" class="refNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit30" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201502467"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Pang, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tang, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Huang, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hart, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tam, K.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The most widely used oxide for photocatalytic applications owing to its low cost and high activity is TiO2.  The discovery of the photolysis of water on the surface of TiO2 in 1972 launched four decades of intensive research into the underlying chem. and phys. processes involved.  Despite much collected evidence, a thoroughly convincing explanation of why mixed-phase samples of anatase and rutile outperform the individual polymorphs has remained elusive.  One long-standing controversy is the energetic alignment of the band edges of the rutile and anatase polymorphs of TiO2.  We demonstrate, through a combination of state-of-the-art materials simulation techniques and X-ray photoemission expts., that a type-II, staggered, band alignment of ∼ 0.4eV exists between anatase and rutile with anatase possessing the higher electron affinity, or work function.                         cas_cit35R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">35</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synthesis of core-shell-like ZnS/C nanocomposite as improved anode material for lithium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Du, Xuefei; Zhao, Hailei; Lu, Yao; Zhang, Zijia; Kulka, Andrzej; Swierczek, Konrad</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">228</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">100-106</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ELCAAV</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4686</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">ZnS/C nanoparticles with core/shell structure were prepd. by a simple solvothermal process followed by an annealing process.  The core consists of a quite amt. of ultrasmall ZnS nanocrystals (∼10 nm) dispersing in in situ formed C matrix, which is covered by an outer C shell with ∼4 nm thickness.  The nano-sized ZnS crystals effectively shorten the Li ion diffusion paths, while the uniform C shell, together with the inner amorphous C matrix not only provide fast electron conduction, but also act as a buffer matrix to accommodate vol. change occurring on electrochem. cycling.  Such hierarchical-type microstructure is beneficial concerning electrochem. performance of the proposed composite.  When evaluated as an anode material for rechargeable Li ion batteries, the ZnS/C nanocomposite shows a high specific capacity of 741 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 0.1 A g-1 after 300 cycles.  Even at 5 A g-1, a high reversible capacity of 538 mAh g-1 can be still achieved.  The Li diffusion coeff. of ZnS/C electrode is estd. as 6.1 × 10-11 cm2 s-1, contributing to the excellent rate performance of the material.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref36"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref36" class="refNumLink">36</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit36" data-doi="10.1021/am5020953"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xue, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Huang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tang, Y.-B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ng, T.-W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kwong, H.-L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Meng, X.-M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, C.-S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Synthesis of Porous ZnS:Ag<sub>2</sub>S Nanosheets by Ion Exchange for Photocatalytic H<sub>2</sub> Generation</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">ZnS:Ag2S porous nanostructures were prepd. by a simple ion-exchange route using ZnS nanosheets as sacrificial templates.                         cas_cit39R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">39</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Preparation of S/N-codoped carbon nanosheets with tunable interlayer distance for high-rate sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zou, Guoqiang; Hou, Hongshuai; Zhao, Ganggang; Huang, Zhaodong; Ge, Peng; Ji, Xiaobo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Green Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">19</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">19</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4622-4632</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">GRCHFJ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1463-9262</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The conventional strategies for obtaining S/N-codoped carbon materials suffer from a series of problems caused by their complicated exptl. procedures.  Here, S,N-codoped carbon nanosheets were firstly prepd. by a solvent-free one-pot method, displaying an ultra-thin sheet-like structure, a tunable interlayer distance ranging from 0.37 nm to 0.41 nm, and a large surface area up to 809 m2 g-1.  When they were used as an anode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), an outstanding sodium-ion storage performance of 380 mA h g-1 was acquired at 100 mA g-1, which can be attributed to the expanded interlayer distance caused by the introduction of the large covalent radius-sulfur.  The initial coulombic efficiency improved to 60.9%, which may benefit from N-doping.  Most importantly, an excellent rate capability of ∼178 mA h g-1 was obsd. at a c.d. of 5 A g-1 after 5000 cycles, which is among best of the state-of-the-art carbon-based SIBs.  Interestingly, the morphol. of the obtained carbon materials can be tuned from bulk to flake by adjusting the sulfur content or temp.  Given this, this work provides a new method to construct co-doped carbon (esp. tri-doped and multi-doped carbon) and shows that the strategy of co-doping of heteroatoms can effectively optimize the nano/microstructure and enhance the rate capability of the carbon materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref40"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref40" class="refNumLink">40</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit40" data-doi="10.1039/C4CE00107A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Guo, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Guo, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jönsson, P.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A novel redox system, poly(α,α'-dithio-3-amino-o-xylene) (PDTAn), has been presented as a cathode material for high-energy secondary lithium batteries.  PDTAn, contg. one six-member ring with S-S bond on the side chain of aniline, has been electrochem. polymd. and chem. polymd. with (NH4)2S2O8 from the monomer of α,α'-dithio-3-amino-o-xylene, resp.  It has been confirmed by IR and in-situ Raman that the main chain of PDTAn is similar to that of polyaniline, and the S-S bond in the moiety of aniline is preserved after polymn.  This system has many advantages, such as high theor. charge d. (∼370 mA-h/g), fast redox process and enhanced reversibility.  The fast redox process and enhanced reversibility are due to the intramol. cleavage and recombination of the S-S bond, and the intramol. electrocatalytic effect of polyaniline chain.  The charge-discharge tests of Li/PDTAn cell show an initial capacity of 225 mA-h/g and the charge efficiency of &gt;80% after the first cycle activation.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref42" class="refNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit42" data-doi="10.1039/c2cp40727b"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Park, M.                         cas_cit42R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">42</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">One-step synthesis of a sulfur-impregnated graphene cathode for lithium-sulfur batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Park, Min-Sik; Yu, Ji-Sang; Kim, Ki Jae; Jeong, Goojin; Kim, Jae-Hun; Jo, Yong-Nam; Hwang, Uk; Kang, Shin; Woo, Taewoo; Kim, Young-Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Chemistry Chemical Physics</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">14</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A practical route is introduced for synthesizing a sulfur-impregnated graphene composite as a promising cathode material for lithium-sulfur batteries.  Sulfur particles with a size of a few microns are successfully grown in the interior spaces between randomly dispersed graphene sheets through a heterogeneous crystal growth mechanism.  The proposed route not only enables the control of the particle size of active sulfur but also affords quant. yields of composite powder in large quantities.  The potential use is investigated of the sulfur-impregnated graphene composite as a cathode material owing to its advantages of confining active sulfur, preventing the dissoln. of sol. polysulfides, and providing sufficient elec. conduction.  A high discharge capacity of 1237 mA h g-1 during the first cycle and a good cyclic retention of 67% after 50 cycles are attained in a voltage range of 1.8-2.6 V vs. Li/Li+.  These results emphasize the importance of tailoring cathode materials for improving the electrochem. properties of lithium-sulfur batteries.  The results provide a basis for further investigations on advanced lithium batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref43" class="refNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit43" data-doi="10.1080/03067310500249930"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yamaguchi, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kumakura, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sugasawa, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Harino, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yamada, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shibata, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Senda, T.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Direct Analysis of Zinc Pyrithione Using LC-MS</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Int.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Several series of activated carbons were prepd. by chem. activation of peach stones with ZnCl2 to show the effect of variables such as precursor particle size, extent of impregnation, impregnation method, and carbonization temp. on the surface area, porosity, and bulk d. of the resulting activated carbons.  The adsorption isotherms of BuH at 273 K on all carbons prepd. were of type I, with a defined plateau, the extent of which was a function of the prepn. conditions.  The main factor affecting the surface area and the micropore size distribution was the amt. of Zn introduced in the precursor during impregnation.  Partial gasification in CO2 of the carbons produced considerable development of surface area and porosity, max. for burn-offs around 60-70%.  It is possible to prep. activated carbons with very high surface area (&gt;3000 m2/g) compatible with a granular form and reasonable bulk d.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref45" class="refNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit45" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2012.02.042"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kucinska, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cyganiuk, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lukaszewicz, J.                         cas_cit46R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">46</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">ZnS-Sb2S3@C Core-Double Shell Polyhedron Structure Derived from Metal-Organic Framework as Anodes for High Performance Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Dong, Shihua; Li, Caixia; Ge, Xiaoli; Li, Zhaoqiang; Miao, Xianguang; Yin, Longwei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Taking advantage of zeolitic imidazolate framework (ZIF-8), ZnS-Sb2S3@C core-double shell polyhedron structure is synthesized through a sulfurization reaction between Zn2+ dissocd. from ZIF-8 and S2- from thioacetamide (TAA), and subsequently a metal cation exchange process between Zn2+ and Sb3+, in which carbon layer is introduced from polymeric resorcinol-formaldehyde to prevent the collapse of the polyhedron.  The polyhedron composite with a ZnS inner-core and Sb2S3/C double-shell as anode for sodium ion batteries (SIBs) shows us a significantly improved electrochem. performance with stable cycle stability, high Coulombic efficiency and specific capacity.  Peculiarly, introducing a carbon shell not only acts as an important protective layer to form a rigid construction and accommodate the vol. changes, but also improves the electronic cond. to optimize the stable cycle performance and the excellent rate property.  The architecture composed of ZnS inner core and a complex Sb2S3/C shell not only facilitates the facile electrolyte infiltration to reduce the Na-ion diffusion length to improve the electrochem. reaction kinetics, but also prevents the structure pulverization caused by Na-ion insertion/extn.  This approach to prep. metal sulfides based on MOFs can be further extended to design other nanostructured systems for high performance energy storage devices.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref47" class="refNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit47" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201700762"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zou, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hou, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qiu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ji, X.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Controllable Interlayer Spacing of Sulfur-Doped Graphitic Carbon Nanosheets for Fast Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Small</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">13</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">1700762</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit48R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">48</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Carbon Encapsulated Tin Oxide Nanocomposites: An Efficient Anode for High Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kalubarme, Ramchandra S.; Lee, Jae-Young; Park, Chan-Jin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">31</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">17226-17237</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The major obstacle in realizing sodium (Na)-ion batteries (NIBs) is the absence of suitable neg. electrodes.  This is because graphite, a com. well known anode material for lithium-ion batteries, cannot be utilized as an insertion host for Na ions due to its large ionic size.  In this study, a simple and cost-effective hydrothermal method to prep. carbon coated tin oxide (SnO2) nanostructures as an efficient anode material for NIBs was reported as a function of the solvent used.  A single phase SnO2 resulted for the ethanol solvent, while a blend of SnO and SnO2 resulted for the DI water and ethylene glycol solvents.  The elemental mapping in the transmission electron microscopy confirmed the presence of carbon coating on the SnO2 nanoparticles.  In cell tests, the anodes of carbon coated SnO2 prepd. in ethanol solvent exhibited stable cycling performance and attained a capacity of about 514 mAh g-1 on the first charge.  With the help of the conductive carbon coating, the SnO2 delivers more capacity at high rates: 304 mAh g-1 at the 1 C rate, 213 mAh g-1 at the 2 C rate and 133 mAh g-1 at the 5 C rate.  The excellent cyclability and high rate capability are the result of the formation of a mixed conducting network and uniform carbon coating on the SnO2 nanoparticles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript" nonce="931887bb8dbf54b8-SJC">window.refViewer={'ref1':['cit1'],'ref2':['cit2'],'ref3':['cit3'],'ref4':['cit4'],'ref5':['cit5'],'ref6':['cit6'],'ref7':['cit7'],'ref8':['cit8'],'ref9':['cit9'],'ref10':['cit10'],'ref11':['cit11'],'ref12':['cit12'],'ref13':['cit13'],'ref14':['cit14'],'ref15':['cit15'],'ref16':['cit16'],'ref17':['cit17'],'ref18':['cit18'],'ref19':['cit19'],'ref20':['cit20'],'ref21':['cit21'],'ref22':['cit22'],'ref23':['cit23'],'ref24':['cit24'],'ref25':['cit25'],'ref26':['cit26'],'ref27':['cit27'],'ref28':['cit28'],'ref29':['cit29'],'ref30':['cit30'],'ref31':['cit31'],'ref32':['cit32'],'ref33':['cit33'],'ref34':['cit34'],'ref35':['cit35'],'ref36':['cit36'],'ref37':['cit37'],'ref38':['cit38'],'ref39':['cit39'],'ref40':['cit40'],'ref41':['cit41'],'ref42':['cit42'],'ref43':['cit43'],'ref44':['cit44'],'ref45':['cit45'],'ref46':['cit46'],'ref47':['cit47'],'ref48':['cit48']}</script><div class="articleCitedByDropzone" data-pb-dropzone="articleCitedByDropzone"><div class="cited-by"><div class="article_content-header cited-content_header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div tabindex="0" class="article_content-title"><h2 id="citeThis">Cited By</h2><div class="article__copy"> <button class="article__copy__btn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><div class="cited-by__content"><div class="sciteMetricsDropzone">

                        rightTab-cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Pyrite (FeS2) nanocrystals as inexpensive high-performance lithium-ion cathode and sodium-ion anode materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Walter, Marc; Zund, Tanja; Kovalenko, Maksym V.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nanoscale</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">In light of the impeding depletion of fossil fuels and necessity to lower carbon dioxide emissions, economically viable high-performance batteries are urgently needed for numerous applications ranging from elec. cars to stationary large-scale electricity storage.  Due to its low raw material cost, non-toxicity and potentially high charge-storage capacity pyrite (FeS2) is a highly promising material for such next-generation batteries.  In this work we present the electrochem. performance of FeS2 nanocrystals (NCs) as lithium-ion and sodium-ion storage materials.  First, we show that nanoscopic FeS2 is a promising lithium-ion cathode material, delivering a capacity of 715 mA h g-1 and av. energy d. of 1237 Wh kg-1 for 100 cycles, twice higher than for commonly used LiCoO2 cathodes.  Then we demonstrate, for the first time, that FeS2 NCs can serve as highly reversible sodium-ion anode material with long cycling life.  As sodium-ion anode material, FeS2 NCs provide capacities above 500 mA h g-1 for 400 cycles at a current rate of 1000 mA g-1.  In all our tests and control expts., the performance of chem. synthesized nanoscale FeS2 clearly surpasses bulk FeS2 as well as large no. of other nanostructured metal sulfides.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2016.06.019"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Song, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bai, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yan, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sun, X.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Morphology-Dependent Performance of Nanostructured Ni<sub>3</sub>S<sub>2</sub>/Ni Anode Electrodes for High Performance Sodium Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nano Energy</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">26</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">533</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">540</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref13" class="rightTabRefNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit13" data-doi="10.1021/am504310k"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hou, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jing, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fang, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Song, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Pan, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ji, X.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium/Lithium Storage Behavior of Antimony Hollow Nanospheres for Rechargeable Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries have come up as an alternative to lithium-ion batteries for large-scale applications because of abundant Na storage in the earth crust.  Antimony hollow nanospheres obtained by galvanic replacement were first applied as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries and exhibited superior electrochem. performances with high reversible capacity of 622.2 mA-h/g at a c.d. of 50 mA/g after 50 cycles, close to the theor. capacity (660 mA-h/g); even at high c.d. of 1600 mA/g, the reversible capacities can also reach 315 mA-h/g.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Maintaining structural stability is a great challenge for high-capacity conversion electrodes with large vol. change but is necessary for the development of high-energy-d., long-cycling batteries.  Here, a stable phosphorus anode is reported for sodium ion batteries by the synergistic use of chem. bonded phosphorus-carbon nanotube (P-CNT) hybrid and cross-linked polymer binder.  The P-CNT hybrid was synthesized through ball-milling of red phosphorus and carboxylic group functionalized carbon nanotubes.  The P-O-C bonds formed in this process help maintain contact between phosphorus and CNTs, leading to a durable hybrid.  In addn., cross-linked CM-cellulose-citric acid binder was used to form a robust electrode.  As a result, this anode delivers a stable cycling capacity of 1586.2 mAh/g after 100 cycles, along with high initial Coulombic efficiency of 84.7% and subsequent cycling efficiency of ∼99%.  The unique electrode framework through chem. bonding strategy reported here is potentially inspirable for other electrode materials with large vol. change in use.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref15" class="rightTabRefNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit15" data-doi="10.1039/C5CC02564H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shadike, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ding, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sang, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fu, Z.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Improved Electrochemical Performance of CoS<sub>2</sub>–MWCNT Nanocomposites for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A facile self-sponsored doping approach is developed to synthesize S and N co-doped trimodal-porous structured graphitic carbon network electrocatalysts.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The most widely used oxide for photocatalytic applications owing to its low cost and high activity is TiO2.  The discovery of the photolysis of water on the surface of TiO2 in 1972 launched four decades of intensive research into the underlying chem. and phys. processes involved.  Despite much collected evidence, a thoroughly convincing explanation of why mixed-phase samples of anatase and rutile outperform the individual polymorphs has remained elusive.  One long-standing controversy is the energetic alignment of the band edges of the rutile and anatase polymorphs of TiO2.  We demonstrate, through a combination of state-of-the-art materials simulation techniques and X-ray photoemission expts., that a type-II, staggered, band alignment of ∼ 0.4eV exists between anatase and rutile with anatase possessing the higher electron affinity, or work function.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A novel redox system, poly(α,α'-dithio-3-amino-o-xylene) (PDTAn), has been presented as a cathode material for high-energy secondary lithium batteries. The wet chitosan paste is heat treated and transformed into a nonporous solid i.e. a carbon-rich matrix with uniformly suspended Na2CO3 nanocrystallites.  Subsequent HCl etching opens cavities in the matrix due to the removal of the Na2CO3.  The active carbon is high surface area (above 400 m2/g), microporous (below 1 nm) and nitrogen-rich (above 6% at.).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref46"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref46" class="rightTabRefNumLink">46</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit46" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.7b03321"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dong, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ge, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Miao, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yin, L.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">ZnS-Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@C Core-Double Shell Polyhedron Structure Derived from Metal–Organic Framework as Anodes for High Performance Sodium Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Nano</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">11</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">6474</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">6482</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acsnano.7b03321</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit46R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">46</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">ZnS-Sb2S3@C Core-Double Shell Polyhedron Structure Derived from Metal-Organic Framework as Anodes for High Performance Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Dong, Shihua; Li, Caixia; Ge, Xiaoli; Li, Zhaoqiang; Miao, Xianguang; Yin, Longwei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6474-6482</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ANCAC3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1936-0851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Taking advantage of zeolitic imidazolate framework (ZIF-8), ZnS-Sb2S3@C core-double shell polyhedron structure is synthesized through a sulfurization reaction between Zn2+ dissocd. from ZIF-8 and S2- from thioacetamide (TAA), and subsequently a metal cation exchange process between Zn2+ and Sb3+, in which carbon layer is introduced from polymeric resorcinol-formaldehyde to prevent the collapse of the polyhedron.  The polyhedron composite with a ZnS inner-core and Sb2S3/C double-shell as anode for sodium ion batteries (SIBs) shows us a significantly improved electrochem. performance with stable cycle stability, high Coulombic efficiency and specific capacity.  Peculiarly, introducing a carbon shell not only acts as an important protective layer to form a rigid construction and accommodate the vol. changes, but also improves the electronic cond. to optimize the stable cycle performance and the excellent rate property.  The architecture composed of ZnS inner core and a complex Sb2S3/C shell not only facilitates the facile electrolyte infiltration to reduce the Na-ion diffusion length to improve the electrochem. reaction kinetics, but also prevents the structure pulverization caused by Na-ion insertion/extn.  This approach to prep. metal sulfides based on MOFs can be further extended to design other nanostructured systems for high performance energy storage devices.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref47" class="rightTabRefNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit47" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201700762"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zou, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hou, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qiu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ji, X.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Controllable Interlayer Spacing of Sulfur-Doped Graphitic Carbon Nanosheets for Fast Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Small</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">13</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">1700762</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/smll.201700762</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            rightTab-cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref48" class="rightTabRefNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit48" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.5b04178"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kalubarme, R. S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, J.-Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Park, C.-J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Carbon Encapsulated Tin Oxide Nanocomposites: An Efficient Anode for High Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">7</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">17226</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">17237</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acsami.5b04178</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit48R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">48</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Carbon Encapsulated Tin Oxide Nanocomposites: An Efficient Anode for High Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kalubarme, Ramchandra S.; Lee, Jae-Young; Park, Chan-Jin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">31</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">17226-17237</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The major obstacle in realizing sodium (Na)-ion batteries (NIBs) is the absence of suitable neg. electrodes.  This is because graphite, a com. well known anode material for lithium-ion batteries, cannot be utilized as an insertion host for Na ions due to its large ionic size.  In this study, a simple and cost-effective hydrothermal method to prep. carbon coated tin oxide (SnO2) nanostructures as an efficient anode material for NIBs was reported as a function of the solvent used.  A single phase SnO2 resulted for the ethanol solvent, while a blend of SnO and SnO2 resulted for the DI water and ethylene glycol solvents.  The elemental mapping in the transmission electron microscopy confirmed the presence of carbon coating on the SnO2 nanoparticles.  In cell tests, the anodes of carbon coated SnO2 prepd. in ethanol solvent exhibited stable cycling performance and attained a capacity of about 514 mAh g-1 on the first charge.  With the help of the conductive carbon coating, the SnO2 delivers more capacity at high rates: 304 mAh g-1 at the 1 C rate, 213 mAh g-1 at the 2 C rate and 133 mAh g-1 at the 5 C rate.  The excellent cyclability and high rate capability are the result of the formation of a mixed conducting network and uniform carbon coating on the SnO2 nanoparticles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i10">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.7b15659" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.7b15659</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM images of samples obtained at different heat-treatment temperatures and after cycles; cycling performances of samples under various heat-treatment temperatures; EDX spectra, element mapping, and N 1s XPS of ZnS/NSC and A-ZnS/NSC; and FT-IR spectra of zinc pyrithione, ZnS/NSC, and A-ZnS/NSC (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/suppl_file/am7b15659_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b15659/suppl_file/am7b15659_si_001.pdf">am7b15659_si_001.pdf (1.45 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

	    var label = '';

	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
